class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n<div id="mw-indicator-pp-default" class="mw-indicator"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Protection_policy#semi" title="This article is semi-protected."><img alt="Page //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Padlock-silver.svg/40px-Padlock-silver.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Incandescent light bulb</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png/170px-Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png" width="170" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png/255px-Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png/340px-Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="1276" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 230-volt incandescent light bulb with a medium-sized <a href="/wiki/Edison_screw" title="Edison screw">E27</a> (Edison 27 millimeter) <a href="/wiki/Gender_of_connectors_and_fasteners" title="Gender of connectors and fasteners">male screw</a> base. The filament is visible as the mostly horizontal line between the vertical supply wires.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tungsten_filament.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Tungsten_filament.JPG/220px-Tungsten_filament.JPG" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Tungsten_filament.JPG/330px-Tungsten_filament.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Tungsten_filament.JPG/440px-Tungsten_filament.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="717" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tungsten_filament.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscope" title="Scanning electron microscope">SEM</a> image of the <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> filament of an incandescent light bulb.</div></div></div>\n<p>An <b>incandescent light bulb</b>, <b>incandescent lamp</b> or <b>incandescent light globe</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Electric_light" title="Electric light">electric light</a> with a wire <a href="#Filament">filament</a> heated to such a high temperature that it glows with visible light (<a href="/wiki/Incandescence" title="Incandescence">incandescence</a>). The filament is protected from <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> with a glass or <a href="/wiki/Fused_quartz" title="Fused quartz">fused quartz</a> bulb that is filled with <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a> or a vacuum. In a <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">halogen lamp</a>, filament <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> is slowed by a chemical process that redeposits metal vapor onto the filament, thereby extending its life. \n</p><p>The light bulb is supplied with electric current by feed-through terminals or wires embedded in the glass. Most bulbs are used in a socket which provides mechanical support and electrical connections.\n</p><p>Incandescent bulbs are manufactured in a wide range of sizes, light output, and <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a> ratings, from 1.5 volts to about 300 volts. They require no external <a href="/wiki/Regulator_(automatic_control)" title="Regulator (automatic control)">regulating equipment</a>, have low <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_cost" title="Manufacturing cost">manufacturing costs</a>, and work equally well on either alternating current or direct current. As a result, the incandescent bulb is widely used in household and commercial lighting, for portable lighting such as table lamps, car <a href="/wiki/Headlamp" title="Headlamp">headlamps</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flashlight" title="Flashlight">flashlights</a>, and for decorative and advertising lighting.\n</p><p>Incandescent bulbs are much less efficient than other types of electric lighting; incandescent bulbs convert less than 5% of the energy they use into visible light,<sup id="cite_ref-incandescent_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-incandescent-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> with standard light bulbs averaging about 2.2%.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The remaining energy is converted into heat. The <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">luminous efficacy</a> of a typical incandescent bulb is 16 <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">lumens</a> per watt, compared with 60&#160;lm/W for a <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">compact fluorescent</a> bulb or 150&#160;lm/W for some white <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">LED lamps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Some applications of the incandescent bulb (such as <a href="/wiki/Infrared_lamp" title="Infrared lamp">heat lamps</a>) deliberately use the heat generated by the filament. Such applications include <a href="/wiki/Incubator_(egg)" title="Incubator (egg)">incubators</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brooding" class="mw-redirect" title="Brooding">brooding boxes</a> for <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> heat lights for <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptile</a> <a href="/wiki/Terrarium" title="Terrarium">tanks</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Infrared_heater" title="Infrared heater">infrared heating</a> for industrial heating and drying processes, <a href="/wiki/Lava_lamp" title="Lava lamp">lava lamps</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Easy-Bake_Oven" title="Easy-Bake Oven">Easy-Bake Oven</a> toy. Incandescent bulbs typically have short lifetimes compared with other types of lighting; around 1,000 hours for home light bulbs versus typically 10,000 hours for compact fluorescents and 30,000 hours for lighting LEDs.\n</p><p>Incandescent bulbs have been replaced in many applications by other types of electric light, such as <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">compact fluorescent lamps</a> (CFL), <a href="/wiki/Cold_cathode" title="Cold cathode">cold cathode fluorescent lamps</a> (CCFL), <a href="/wiki/High-intensity_discharge_lamp" title="High-intensity discharge lamp">high-intensity discharge lamps</a>, and <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">light-emitting diode lamps</a> (LED). Some jurisdictions, such as the European Union, China, Canada and United States, are in the process<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (June 2018)">needs update</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> of <a href="/wiki/Phase-out_of_incandescent_light_bulbs" title="Phase-out of incandescent light bulbs">phasing out the use of incandescent light bulbs</a> while others, including Colombia,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Mexico, Cuba, Argentina  and Brazil,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> have prohibited them already.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Light output and lifetime</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<p>In addressing the question of who invented the incandescent lamp, historians Robert Friedel and Paul Israel list 22 inventors of incandescent lamps prior to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Swan" title="Joseph Swan">Joseph Swan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriedelIsrael2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8U-Naf4DuzMCpgPA115_115\xe2\x80\x93117&#93;_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriedelIsrael2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8U-Naf4DuzMCpgPA115_115\xe2\x80\x93117]-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> They conclude that Edison\'s version was able to outstrip the others because of a combination of three factors: an effective <a href="/wiki/Incandescent" class="mw-redirect" title="Incandescent">incandescent</a> material, a higher <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> than others were able to achieve (by use of the <a href="/wiki/Sprengel_pump" title="Sprengel pump">Sprengel pump</a>) and a high <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical resistance">resistance</a> that made power distribution from a centralized source economically viable.\n</p><p>Historian Thomas Hughes has attributed Edison\'s success to his development of an entire, integrated system of electric lighting.\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The lamp was a small component in his system of electric lighting, and no more critical to its effective functioning than the Edison Jumbo <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical generator">generator</a>, the Edison main and feeder, and the parallel-distribution system. Other inventors with generators and incandescent lamps, and with comparable ingenuity and excellence, have long been forgotten because their creators did not preside over their introduction in a system of <a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">lighting</a>.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Thomas P. Hughes, In <i>Technology at the Turning Point</i>, edited by W. B. Pickett<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<table class="toccolours collapsible collapsed" style="float:right; text-align:center; margin-left:1em;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Timeline of the early evolution of the light bulb<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n<div class="timeline-wrapper"><map name="timeline_0dd6416bab5e280829ed3098e68e4cd4"><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" coords="83,1337,202,1357" title="Irving Langmuir" alt="Irving Langmuir" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/William_David_Coolidge" coords="83,1303,241,1324" title="William David Coolidge" alt="William David Coolidge" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Franjo_Hanaman" coords="83,1237,158,1258" title="Franjo Hanaman" alt="Franjo Hanaman" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" coords="83,1104,191,1125" title="Thomas Edison" alt="Thomas Edison" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" coords="83,1038,191,1059" title="Thomas Edison" alt="Thomas Edison" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Lewis_Howard_Latimer" coords="83,994,230,1014" title="Lewis Howard Latimer" alt="Lewis Howard Latimer" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Hiram_Maxim" coords="83,983,180,1003" title="Hiram Maxim" alt="Hiram Maxim" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" coords="83,972,191,992" title="Thomas Edison" alt="Thomas Edison" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Woodward_&amp;_Evans" coords="83,916,230,937" title="Woodward &amp; Evans" alt="Woodward &amp; Evans" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Wilson_Swan" coords="83,894,219,915" title="Joseph Wilson Swan" alt="Joseph Wilson Swan" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Frederick_de_Moleyns" coords="83,540,230,561" title="Frederick de Moleyns" alt="Frederick de Moleyns" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Warren_De_la_Rue" coords="83,529,208,550" title="Warren De la Rue" alt="Warren De la Rue" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/James_Bowman_Lindsay" coords="83,474,230,494" title="James Bowman Lindsay" alt="James Bowman Lindsay" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Sir_Humphry_Davy" coords="83,186,208,207" title="Sir Humphry Davy" alt="Sir Humphry Davy" /><area shape="rect" href="/wiki/Sir_Humphry_Davy" coords="83,98,208,118" title="Sir Humphry Davy" alt="Sir Humphry Davy" /></map><img usemap="#timeline_0dd6416bab5e280829ed3098e68e4cd4" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/0dd6416bab5e280829ed3098e68e4cd4.png" /></div>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_pre-commercial_research">Early pre-commercial research</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg/170px-Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg/255px-Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg/340px-Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="575" data-file-height="862" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Original carbon-filament bulb from <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a>\'s shop in Menlo Park</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1761 <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Kinnersley" title="Ebenezer Kinnersley">Ebenezer Kinnersley</a> demonstrated heating a wire to incandescence.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1802, <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> used what he described as "<i>a <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battery (electricity)">battery</a> of immense size</i>",<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> consisting of 2,000 cells housed in the basement of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a> of Great Britain,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> to create an incandescent light by passing the current through a thin strip of <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, chosen because the metal had an extremely high <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a>. It was not bright enough nor did it last long enough to be practical, but it was the precedent behind the efforts of scores of experimenters over the next 75 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Davis_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Over the first three-quarters of the 19th century, many experimenters worked with various combinations of platinum or iridium wires, carbon rods, and evacuated or semi-evacuated enclosures. Many of these devices were demonstrated and some were patented.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoustonKennely1896chapter_2_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoustonKennely1896chapter_2-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1835, <a href="/wiki/James_Bowman_Lindsay" title="James Bowman Lindsay">James Bowman Lindsay</a> demonstrated a constant electric light at a public meeting in <a href="/wiki/Dundee,_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Dundee, Scotland">Dundee, Scotland</a>. He stated that he could "read a book at a distance of one and a half feet". Lindsay, a lecturer at the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Watt_Institution_dundee&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Watt Institution dundee (page does not exist)">Watt Institution</a> in Dundee, Scotland, at the time, had developed a light that was not combustible, created no smoke or smell and was less expensive to produce than Davy\'s platinum-dependent bulb.<sup id="cite_ref-Challoner_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Challoner-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> However, having perfected the device to his own satisfaction, he turned to the problem of <a href="/wiki/Wireless_telegraphy" title="Wireless telegraphy">wireless telegraphy</a> and did not develop the electric light any further. His claims are not well documented, although he is credited in <i>Challoner et al.</i> with being the inventor of the "Incandescent Light Bulb".<sup id="cite_ref-Challoner_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Challoner-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1838, Belgian lithographer <a href="/wiki/Marcellin_Jobard" title="Marcellin Jobard">Marcellin Jobard</a> invented an incandescent light bulb with a vacuum atmosphere using a carbon filament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriedelIsrael2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8U-Naf4DuzMCpgPA91_91&#93;_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriedelIsrael2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8U-Naf4DuzMCpgPA91_91]-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1840, British scientist <a href="/wiki/Warren_de_la_Rue" class="mw-redirect" title="Warren de la Rue">Warren de la Rue</a> enclosed a coiled platinum filament in a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> tube and passed an electric current through it. The design was based on the concept that the high melting point of platinum would allow it to operate at high temperatures and that the evacuated chamber would contain fewer gas molecules to react with the platinum, improving its longevity. Although a workable design, the cost of the platinum made it impractical for commercial use.\n</p><p>In 1841, Frederick de Moleyns of England was granted the first <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patent</a> for an incandescent lamp, with a design using platinum wires contained within a vacuum bulb. He also used carbon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoustonKennely189624_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoustonKennely189624-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriedelIsrael2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8U-Naf4DuzMCpgPA7_7&#93;_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriedelIsrael2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8U-Naf4DuzMCpgPA7_7]-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1845, American <a href="/wiki/John_Wellington_Starr" title="John Wellington Starr">John W. Starr</a> acquired a patent for his incandescent light bulb involving the use of carbon filaments.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> He died shortly after obtaining the patent, and his invention was never produced commercially. Little else is known about him.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1851, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Eug%C3%A8ne_Robert-Houdin" title="Jean Eug\xc3\xa8ne Robert-Houdin">Jean Eug\xc3\xa8ne Robert-Houdin</a> publicly demonstrated incandescent light bulbs on his estate in Blois, France. His light bulbs are on display in the museum of the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Blois" title="Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Blois">Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Blois</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1859, <a href="/wiki/Moses_G._Farmer" title="Moses G. Farmer">Moses G. Farmer</a> built an electric incandescent light bulb using a platinum filament.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> He later patented a light bulb which was purchased by Thomas Edison.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stamp_of_USSR_1634g.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Stamp_of_USSR_1634g.jpg/170px-Stamp_of_USSR_1634g.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Stamp_of_USSR_1634g.jpg/255px-Stamp_of_USSR_1634g.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Stamp_of_USSR_1634g.jpg/340px-Stamp_of_USSR_1634g.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1329" data-file-height="1785" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stamp_of_USSR_1634g.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Lodygin" title="Alexander Lodygin">Alexander Lodygin</a> on 1951 Soviet postal stamp</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1872, Russian <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Nikolayevich_Lodygin" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander Nikolayevich Lodygin">Alexander Lodygin</a> invented an incandescent light bulb and obtained a Russian patent in 1874. He used as a burner two carbon rods of diminished section in a glass receiver, hermetically sealed, and filled with nitrogen, electrically arranged so that the current could be passed to the second carbon when the first had been consumed.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Later he lived in the US, changed his name to Alexander de Lodyguine and applied and obtained patents for incandescent lamps having <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhodium" title="Rhodium">rhodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> filaments,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and a bulb using a molybdenum filament was demonstrated at the <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1900)" title="Exposition Universelle (1900)">world fair</a> of 1900 in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 24 July 1874, a Canadian patent was filed by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Woodward_(inventor)" title="Henry Woodward (inventor)">Henry Woodward</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathew_Evans" title="Mathew Evans">Mathew Evans</a> for a lamp consisting of carbon rods mounted in a nitrogen-filled glass cylinder. They were unsuccessful at commercializing their lamp, and sold rights to their patent (<span><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US181613">U.S. Patent 0,181,613</a></span>) to Thomas Edison in 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Heinrich_G%C3%B6bel" title="Heinrich G\xc3\xb6bel">Heinrich G\xc3\xb6bel</a> in 1893 claimed he had designed the first incandescent light bulb in 1854, with a thin carbonized <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> filament of high resistance, platinum lead-in wires in an all-glass envelope, and a high vacuum. Judges of four courts raised doubts about the alleged G\xc3\xb6bel anticipation, but there was never a decision in a final hearing due to the expiry date of Edison\'s patent. A research work published 2007 concluded that the story of the G\xc3\xb6bel lamps in the 1850s is a legend.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercialization">Commercialization</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dominance_of_carbon_filament_and_vacuum">Dominance of carbon filament and vacuum</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbonfilament.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Carbonfilament.jpg/220px-Carbonfilament.jpg" width="220" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Carbonfilament.jpg/330px-Carbonfilament.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Carbonfilament.jpg/440px-Carbonfilament.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1061" data-file-height="914" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbonfilament.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carbon filament lamps, showing darkening of bulb</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Wilson_Swan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Joseph_Wilson_Swan.jpg/170px-Joseph_Wilson_Swan.jpg" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Joseph_Wilson_Swan.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="194" data-file-height="275" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Wilson_Swan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sir_Joseph_Wilson_Swan" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Joseph Wilson Swan">Sir Joseph Wilson Swan</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Swan" title="Joseph Swan">Joseph Swan</a> (1828\xe2\x80\x931914) was a British physicist and chemist. In 1850, he began working with carbonized paper filaments in an evacuated glass bulb. By 1860, he was able to demonstrate a working device but the lack of a good vacuum and an adequate supply of electricity resulted in a short lifetime for the bulb and an inefficient source of light. By the mid-1870s better pumps became available, and Swan returned to his experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-1_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-1-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Joseph_Swan_blue_plaque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sir_Joseph_Swan_blue_plaque.jpg/220px-Sir_Joseph_Swan_blue_plaque.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sir_Joseph_Swan_blue_plaque.jpg/330px-Sir_Joseph_Swan_blue_plaque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sir_Joseph_Swan_blue_plaque.jpg/440px-Sir_Joseph_Swan_blue_plaque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Joseph_Swan_blue_plaque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Historical plaque at <a href="/wiki/Underhill,_Low_Fell,_Gateshead" title="Underhill, Low Fell, Gateshead">Underhill</a>, the first house to be lit by electric lights</div></div></div>\n<p>With the help of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_Stearn&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Charles Stearn (page does not exist)">Charles Stearn</a>, an expert on vacuum pumps, in 1878, Swan developed a method of processing that avoided the early bulb blackening. This received a British Patent in 1880.<sup id="cite_ref-Incandescent_Electric_Lamp_21-25_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Incandescent_Electric_Lamp_21-25-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2014)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Incandescent_light_bulb#Dubious" title="Talk:Incandescent light bulb">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup> On 18 December 1878, a lamp using a slender carbon rod was shown at a meeting of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_Chemical_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Newcastle Chemical Society (page does not exist)">Newcastle Chemical Society</a>, and Swan gave a working demonstration at their meeting on 17 January 1879. It was also shown to 700 who attended a meeting of the <a href="/wiki/Literary_and_Philosophical_Society_of_Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Literary and Philosophical Society of Newcastle upon Tyne">Literary and Philosophical Society of Newcastle upon Tyne</a> on 3 February 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-WIRED_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WIRED-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> These lamps used a carbon rod from an arc lamp rather than a slender filament. Thus they had low resistance and required very large conductors to supply the necessary current, so they were not commercially practical, although they did furnish a demonstration of the possibilities of incandescent lighting with relatively high vacuum, a carbon conductor, and platinum lead-in wires. This bulb lasted about 40 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-WIRED_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WIRED-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Swan then turned his attention to producing a better carbon filament and the means of attaching its ends. He devised a method of treating cotton to produce \'parchmentised thread\' in the early 1880s and obtained British Patent 4933 that same year.<sup id="cite_ref-Incandescent_Electric_Lamp_21-25_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Incandescent_Electric_Lamp_21-25-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> From this year he began installing light bulbs in homes and landmarks in England. His house, <a href="/wiki/Underhill,_Low_Fell,_Gateshead" title="Underhill, Low Fell, Gateshead">Underhill, Low Fell, Gateshead</a>, was the first in the world to be lit by a lightbulb and also the first house in the world to be lit by hydroelectric power. In 1878 the home of <a href="/wiki/William_George_Armstrong,_1st_Baron_Armstrong" class="mw-redirect" title="William George Armstrong, 1st Baron Armstrong">Lord Armstrong</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cragside" title="Cragside">Cragside</a> was also among the first houses to be lit by electricity. In the early 1880s he had started his company.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In 1881, the <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Theatre" title="Savoy Theatre">Savoy Theatre</a> in the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Westminster" title="City of Westminster">City of Westminster</a>, London was lit by Swan incandescent lightbulbs, which was the first theatre, and the first public building in the world, to be lit entirely by electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-Times_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The first street in the world to be lit by an incandescent lightbulb was Mosley Street, <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>. It was lit by Joseph Swan\'s incandescent lamp on 3 February 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg/220px-Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg/330px-Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg/440px-Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg 2x" data-file-width="737" data-file-height="388" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edison carbon filament lamps, early 1880s</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg/170px-Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg/255px-Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg/340px-Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="759" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Alva_Edison" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Alva Edison">Thomas Alva Edison</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a> began serious research into developing a practical incandescent lamp in 1878. Edison filed his first patent application for "Improvement In Electric Lights" on 14 October 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  After many experiments, first with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> in the early 1880s and then with <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> and other metals, in the end Edison returned to a carbon filament.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The first successful test was on 22 October 1879,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and lasted 13.5 hours. Edison continued to improve this design and by 4 November 1879, filed for a US patent for an electric lamp using "a carbon filament or strip coiled and connected ... to platina contact wires."<sup id="cite_ref-Patent898_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patent898-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Although the patent described several ways of creating the carbon filament including using "cotton and linen thread, wood splints, papers coiled in various ways,"<sup id="cite_ref-Patent898_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patent898-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Edison and his team later discovered that a carbonized bamboo filament could last more than 1200 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 1880, the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Railroad_and_Navigation_Company" title="Oregon Railroad and Navigation Company">Oregon Railroad and Navigation Company</a> steamer, <a href="/wiki/SS_Columbia_(1880)" title="SS Columbia (1880)"><i>Columbia</i></a>, became the first application for Edison\'s incandescent electric lamps (it was also the first ship to use a <a href="/wiki/Dynamo" title="Dynamo">dynamo</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dalton_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalton-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Albon_Man" title="Albon Man">Albon Man</a>, a New York lawyer, started <a href="/wiki/Electro-Dynamic_Light_Company" title="Electro-Dynamic Light Company">Electro-Dynamic Light Company</a> in 1878 to exploit his patents and those of <a href="/wiki/William_E._Sawyer" title="William E. Sawyer">William Sawyer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EE1890_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EE1890-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ER1890_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ER1890-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Weeks later the United States Electric Lighting Company was organized.<sup id="cite_ref-EE1890_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EE1890-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ER1890_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ER1890-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WE1890_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WE1890-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> This company didn\'t make their first commercial installation of incandescent lamps until the fall of 1880 at the Mercantile Safe Deposit Company in New York City, about six months after the Edison incandescent lamps had been installed on the <i>Columbia</i>. <a href="/wiki/Hiram_S._Maxim" class="mw-redirect" title="Hiram S. Maxim">Hiram S. Maxim</a> was the chief engineer at the United States Electric Lighting Company.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lewis_Latimer" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewis Latimer">Lewis Latimer</a>, employed at the time by Edison, developed an improved method of heat-treating carbon filaments which reduced breakage and allowed them to be molded into novel shapes, such as the characteristic "M" shape of Maxim filaments. On 17 January 1882, Latimer received a patent for the "Process of Manufacturing Carbons", an improved method for the production of light bulb filaments, which was purchased by the United States Electric Light Company.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Latimer patented other improvements such as a better way of attaching filaments to their wire supports.<sup id="cite_ref-Fouch\xc3\xa9_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fouch\xc3\xa9-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Britain, the Edison and Swan companies merged into the <a href="/wiki/Edison_and_Swan_United_Electric_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Edison and Swan United Electric Company">Edison and Swan United Electric Company</a> (later known as Ediswan, and ultimately incorporated into <a href="/wiki/Thorn_Lighting_Ltd" class="mw-redirect" title="Thorn Lighting Ltd">Thorn Lighting Ltd</a>). Edison was initially against this combination, but after Swan <a href="/wiki/Sued" class="mw-redirect" title="Sued">sued</a> him and won, Edison was eventually forced to cooperate, and the merger was made. Eventually, Edison acquired all of Swan\'s interest in the company. Swan sold his US patent rights to the <a href="/wiki/Brush_Electric_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Brush Electric Company">Brush Electric Company</a> in June 1882.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:138px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Light_bulb.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/06/Light_bulb.png" width="136" height="232" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="136" data-file-height="232" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Light_bulb.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US223898">U.S. Patent 0,223,898</a></span> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a> for an improved electric lamp, 27 January 1880</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="United States Patent and Trademark Office">United States Patent Office</a> gave a ruling 8 October 1883, that Edison\'s patents were based on the prior art of <a href="/wiki/William_E._Sawyer" title="William E. Sawyer">William Sawyer</a> and were invalid. Litigation continued for a number of years. Eventually on 6 October 1889, a judge ruled that Edison\'s electric light improvement claim for "a filament of carbon of high resistance" was valid.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1896 Italian inventor Arturo Malignani (1865\xe2\x80\x931939) patented an evacuation method for mass production, which allowed obtaining economic bulbs lasting 800 hours. The patent was acquired by Edison in 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-1_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-1-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>In 1897, German physicist and chemist <a href="/wiki/Walther_Nernst" title="Walther Nernst">Walther Nernst</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Nernst_lamp" title="Nernst lamp">Nernst lamp</a>, a form of incandescent lamp that used a ceramic <a href="/wiki/Globar" title="Globar">globar</a> and did not require enclosure in a vacuum or inert gas.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Twice as efficient as carbon filament lamps, Nernst lamps were briefly popular until overtaken by lamps using metal filaments.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Revolution_of_the_tungsten_filament.2C_inert_gas.2C_and_the_coiled_coil"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolution_of_the_tungsten_filament,_inert_gas,_and_the_coiled_coil">Revolution of the tungsten filament, inert gas, and the coiled coil</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg/170px-Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg" width="170" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg/255px-Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg 2x" data-file-width="328" data-file-height="525" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hanaman (left) and Dr. Just (right), the inventors of the tungsten bulbs</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tungsram_advertisement.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tungsram_advertisement.jpg/170px-Tungsram_advertisement.jpg" width="170" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tungsram_advertisement.jpg/255px-Tungsram_advertisement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tungsram_advertisement.jpg/340px-Tungsram_advertisement.jpg 2x" data-file-width="364" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tungsram_advertisement.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a> advertising of the <a href="/wiki/Tungsram" title="Tungsram">Tungsram</a>-bulb from 1906. This was the first light bulb that used a filament made from <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> instead of carbon. The inscription reads: <i>wire lamp with a drawn wire \xe2\x80\x93 indestructible</i>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2200K_Spectrum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/2200K_Spectrum.jpg/220px-2200K_Spectrum.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/2200K_Spectrum.jpg/330px-2200K_Spectrum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/2200K_Spectrum.jpg/440px-2200K_Spectrum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4297" data-file-height="2731" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2200K_Spectrum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spectrum of an incandescent lamp at 2200K, showing most of its emission as invisible <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> light.</div></div></div>\n<p>On 13 December 1904, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungarian</a> <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_Just" class="mw-redirect" title="S\xc3\xa1ndor Just">S\xc3\xa1ndor Just</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croats" title="Croats">Croatian</a> <a href="/wiki/Franjo_Hanaman" title="Franjo Hanaman">Franjo Hanaman</a> were granted a Hungarian patent (No. 34541) for a <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> filament lamp that lasted longer and gave brighter light than the carbon filament.<sup id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-1_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-1-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Tungsten filament lamps were first marketed by the <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungarian</a> company <a href="/wiki/Tungsram" title="Tungsram">Tungsram</a> in 1904. This type is often called Tungsram-bulbs in many European countries.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Filling a bulb with an <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> slows down the evaporation of the tungsten filament compared to operating it in a vacuum. This allows for greater temperatures and therefore greater <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">efficacy</a> with less reduction in filament life.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1906, <a href="/wiki/William_D._Coolidge" title="William D. Coolidge">William D. Coolidge</a> developed a method of making "ductile tungsten" from <a href="/wiki/Sintered" class="mw-redirect" title="Sintered">sintered</a> <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> which could be made into filaments while working for <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric Company</a>. By 1911 General Electric began selling incandescent light bulbs with ductile tungsten wire.\n</p><p>In 1913, <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">Irving Langmuir</a> found that filling a lamp with <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a> instead of a vacuum resulted in twice the luminous efficacy and reduction of bulb blackening.\n</p><p>In 1917, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Burnie_Lee_Benbow&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Burnie Lee Benbow (page does not exist)">Burnie Lee Benbow</a> was granted a patent for inventing the coiled coil filament.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In 1921, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Junichi_Miura&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Junichi Miura (page does not exist)">Junichi Miura</a> created the first double-coil bulb using a coiled coil tungsten filament while working for <a href="/wiki/Hakunetsusha" title="Hakunetsusha">Hakunetsusha</a> (a predecessor of <a href="/wiki/Toshiba" title="Toshiba">Toshiba</a>). At the time, machinery to mass-produce coiled coil filaments did not exist. Hakunetsusha developed a method to mass-produce coiled coil filaments by 1936.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 1924 and the outbreak of the Second World War, the <a href="/wiki/Phoebus_cartel" title="Phoebus cartel">Phoebus cartel</a> attempted to fix prices and sales quotas for bulb manufacturers outside of North America.\n</p><p>In 1925, <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Pipkin" title="Marvin Pipkin">Marvin Pipkin</a>, an American chemist, patented a process for <a href="/wiki/Frosting_(decorative_arts)" title="Frosting (decorative arts)">frosting</a> the inside of lamp bulbs without weakening them, and in 1947, he patented a process for coating the inside of lamps with <a href="/wiki/Silica" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica">silica</a>.\n</p><p>In 1930, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Imre_Br%C3%B3dy" title="Imre Br\xc3\xb3dy">Imre Br\xc3\xb3dy</a> filled lamps with krypton gas rather than argon, and designed a process to obtain krypton from air. Production of krypton filled lamps based on his invention started at <a href="/wiki/Ajka" title="Ajka">Ajka</a> in 1937, in a factory co-designed by Pol\xc3\xa1nyi and Hungarian-born physicist <a href="/wiki/Egon_Orowan" title="Egon Orowan">Egon Orowan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1964, improvements in efficiency and production of incandescent lamps had reduced the cost of providing a given quantity of light by a factor of thirty, compared with the cost at introduction of Edison\'s lighting system.<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Consumption of incandescent light bulbs grew rapidly in the US. In 1885, an estimated 300,000 general lighting service lamps were sold, all with carbon filaments. When tungsten filaments were introduced, about 50 million lamp sockets existed in the US. In 1914, 88.5 million lamps were used, (only 15% with carbon filaments), and by 1945, annual sales of lamps were 795 million (more than 5 lamps per person per year).<sup id="cite_ref-Kane2001_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kane2001-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Efficacy.2C_efficiency.2C_and_environmental_impact"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficacy,_efficiency,_and_environmental_impact">Efficacy, efficiency, and environmental impact</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png/170px-Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png" width="170" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png 1.5x" data-file-width="215" data-file-height="366" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Xenon <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">halogen lamp</a> with an E27 base, which can replace a non-halogen bulb</div></div></div>\n<p>Of the power consumed by typical incandescent light bulbs, 95% or more is converted into heat rather than visible light.<sup id="cite_ref-incandescent_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-incandescent-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  Other electrical light sources are more effective.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">Luminous <i>efficacy</i></a> of a light source may be defined in two ways. The radiant luminous efficacy (LER) is the ratio of the visible light flux emitted (the <i>luminous flux</i>) to the total power radiated over all wavelengths. The source luminous efficacy (LES) is the ratio of the visible light flux emitted (the <i>luminous flux</i>) to the total power input to the source, such as a lamp.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Visible light is measured in <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">lumens</a>, a unit which is defined in part by the differing sensitivity of the human eye to different wavelengths of light. Not all wavelengths of visible electromagnetic energy are equally effective at stimulating the human eye; the luminous efficacy of radiant energy (LER) is a measure of how well the distribution of energy matches the perception of the eye. The units of luminous efficacy are "lumens per watt" (lpw). The maximum LER possible is 683&#160;lm/W for monochromatic green light at 555 nanometers wavelength, the peak sensitivity of the human eye.\n</p><p>The luminous <i>efficiency</i> is defined as the ratio of the luminous efficacy to the theoretical maximum luminous efficacy of 683 lpw, and, as for luminous efficacy, is of two types, radiant luminous efficiency (LFR) and source luminous efficacy (LFS).<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The chart below lists values of overall luminous efficacy and efficiency for several types of general service, 120-volt, 1000-hour lifespan incandescent bulb, and several idealized light sources. The values for the incandescent bulbs are source efficiencies and efficacies. The values for the ideal sources are radiant efficiencies and efficacies. A similar chart in the article on <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy#Overall_luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">luminous efficacy</a> compares a broader array of light sources to one another.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Type</th>\n<th>Overall luminous efficiency</th>\n<th>Overall luminous efficacy (lm/W)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>40 W tungsten incandescent\n</td>\n<td>1.9%\n</td>\n<td>12.6<sup id="cite_ref-incandescent_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-incandescent-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>60 W tungsten incandescent\n</td>\n<td>2.1%\n</td>\n<td>14.5<sup id="cite_ref-incandescent_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-incandescent-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>100 W tungsten incandescent\n</td>\n<td>2.6%\n</td>\n<td>17.5<sup id="cite_ref-incandescent_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-incandescent-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>glass halogen\n</td>\n<td>2.3%\n</td>\n<td>16\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>quartz halogen\n</td>\n<td>3.5%\n</td>\n<td>24\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>photographic and projection lamps with very high filament temperatures and short lifetimes\n</td>\n<td>5.1%\n</td>\n<td>35<sup id="cite_ref-bulbguide_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bulbguide-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>ideal <a href="/wiki/Black-body" class="mw-redirect" title="Black-body">black-body</a> radiator at 4000&#160;K (or a <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">class</a> K star like <a href="/wiki/Arcturus" title="Arcturus">Arcturus</a>)\n</td>\n<td>7.0%\n</td>\n<td>47.5\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>ideal black-body radiator at 7000&#160;K (or a <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">class</a> F star like <a href="/wiki/Procyon" title="Procyon">Procyon</a>)\n</td>\n<td>14%\n</td>\n<td>95\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>ideal monochromatic 555&#160;nm (green) source\n</td>\n<td>100%\n</td>\n<td>683<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The spectrum emitted by a <a href="/wiki/Blackbody" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackbody">blackbody</a> radiator at temperatures of incandescent bulbs does not match the sensitivity characteristics of the human eye. Most of the radiation is not in the range of wavelengths to which the eye is sensitive. Tungsten filaments radiate mostly infrared radiation at temperatures where they remain solid \xe2\x80\x93 below 3,695&#160;K (3,422&#160;\xc2\xb0C; 6,191&#160;\xc2\xb0F). Donald L. Klipstein explains it this way: "An ideal thermal radiator produces visible light most efficiently at temperatures around 6,300&#160;\xc2\xb0C (6,600&#160;K; 11,400&#160;\xc2\xb0F). Even at this high temperature, a lot of the radiation is either infrared or ultraviolet, and the theoretical luminous efficacy (LER) is 95 lumens per watt."<sup id="cite_ref-bulbguide_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bulbguide-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> No known material can be used as a filament at this ideal temperature, which is hotter than the sun\'s surface. An upper limit for incandescent lamp luminous efficacy (LER) is around 52 lumens per watt, the theoretical value emitted by tungsten at its melting point.<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although inefficient, incandescent light bulbs have an advantage in applications where accurate color reproduction is important, since the continuous blackbody spectrum emitted from an incandescent light-bulb filament yields near-perfect color rendition, with a <a href="/wiki/Color_rendering_index" title="Color rendering index">color rendering index</a> of 100 (the best possible).<sup id="cite_ref-Edison_Incandescent_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edison_Incandescent-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> White-balancing is still required to avoid too "warm" or "cool" colors, but this is a simple process that requires only the color temperature in <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvins</a> as input for modern, digital visual reproduction equipment such as video or still cameras unless it is completely automated. The color-rendering performance of incandescent lights cannot be matched by LEDs or fluorescent lights, although they can offer satisfactory performance for non-critical applications such as home lighting.<sup id="cite_ref-Edison_LED_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edison_LED-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Edison_Fluorescent_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edison_Fluorescent-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> White-balancing such lights is therefore more complicated, requiring additional adjustments to reduce for example green-magenta color casts, and even when properly white-balanced, the color reproduction will not be perfect.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thermal_image_of_an_incandescent_light.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Thermal_image_of_an_incandescent_light.jpg/220px-Thermal_image_of_an_incandescent_light.jpg" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Thermal_image_of_an_incandescent_light.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="240" data-file-height="211" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thermal_image_of_an_incandescent_light.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thermal image of an incandescent bulb. 71\xe2\x80\x93347&#160;\xc2\xb0F = 22\xe2\x80\x93175&#160;\xc2\xb0C.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png/220px-Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png/330px-Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png/440px-Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png 2x" data-file-width="1201" data-file-height="901" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spectral power distribution of a 25 W incandescent light bulb.</div></div></div>\n<p>For a given quantity of light, an incandescent light bulb produces more heat (and thus consumes more power) than a <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamp</a>. In buildings where <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> is used, incandescent lamps\' heat output increases load on the air conditioning system.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> While heat from lights will reduce the need for running a building\'s heating system, in general a heating system can provide the same amount of heat at a lower cost than incandescent lights.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">Halogen incandescent lamps</a> have higher efficacy, which will allow a halogen light to use less power to produce the same amount of light compared to a non-halogen incandescent light. Halogen lights produce a more constant light-output over time, without much dimming.<sup id="cite_ref-Edison_Halogen_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edison_Halogen-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are many non-incandescent light sources, such as the fluorescent lamp, <a href="/wiki/High-intensity_discharge_lamp" title="High-intensity discharge lamp">high-intensity discharge lamps</a> and <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">LED lamps</a>, which have higher luminous efficiency, and some have been designed to be <a href="/wiki/Retrofitted" class="mw-redirect" title="Retrofitted">retrofitted</a> in fixtures for incandescent lights. These devices produce light by <a href="/wiki/Luminescence" title="Luminescence">luminescence</a>. These lamps produce discrete <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral lines</a> and do not have the broad "tail" of invisible infrared emissions. By careful selection of which electron energy level transitions are used, and fluorescent coatings which modify the spectral distribution, the spectrum emitted can be tuned to mimic the appearance of incandescent sources, or other different <a href="/wiki/Color_temperature" title="Color temperature">color temperatures</a> of white light. Due to the discrete spectral lines rather than a continuous spectrum, the light is not ideal for applications such as photography and cinematography.<sup id="cite_ref-Edison_LED_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edison_LED-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Edison_Fluorescent_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edison_Fluorescent-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost_of_lighting">Cost of lighting</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Architectural_lighting_design" title="Architectural lighting design">Architectural lighting design</a></div>\n<p>The initial cost of an incandescent bulb is small compared to the cost of the energy it uses over its lifetime. Incandescent bulbs have a shorter life than most other lighting, an important factor if replacement is inconvenient or expensive. Some types of lamp, including incandescent and fluorescent, emit less light as they age; this may be an inconvenience, or may reduce effective lifetime due to lamp replacement before total failure. A comparison of incandescent lamp operating cost with other light sources must include illumination requirements, cost of the lamp and labor cost to replace lamps (taking into account effective lamp lifetime), cost of electricity used, effect of lamp operation on heating and air conditioning systems. When used for lighting in houses and commercial buildings, the energy lost to heat can significantly increase the energy required by a building\'s <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> system. During the heating season heat produced by the bulbs is not wasted,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> although in most cases it is more cost effective to obtain heat from the heating system. Regardless, over the course of a year a more efficient lighting system saves energy in nearly all climates.<sup id="cite_ref-cmhc_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cmhc-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Measures_to_ban_use">Measures to ban use</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phase-out_of_incandescent_light_bulbs" title="Phase-out of incandescent light bulbs">Phase-out of incandescent light bulbs</a></div>\n<p>Since incandescent light bulbs use more energy than alternatives such as <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">CFLs</a> and <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">LED lamps</a>, many governments have introduced measures to ban their use, by setting minimum efficacy standards higher than can be achieved by incandescent lamps. Measures to ban light bulbs have been implemented in the European Union, the United States, Russia, Brazil, Argentina, Canada and Australia, among others. In the Europe the EC has calculated that the ban contributes 5 to 10 billion euros to the economy and saves 40 TWh of electricity every year, translating in CO2 emission reductions of 15 million tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the US, federal law has scheduled the most common incandescent light bulbs to be phased out by 2014, to be replaced with more energy-efficient light bulbs.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Traditional incandescent light bulbs were phased out in Australia in November 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Objections to banning the use of incandescent light bulbs include the higher initial cost of alternatives and lower quality of light of fluorescent lamps.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Some people have concerns about the <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamps_and_health" title="Fluorescent lamps and health">health effects</a> of fluorescent lamps. However, even though they contain <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, the environmental performance of CFLs is much better than that of light bulbs, mostly because they consume much less energy and therefore strongly reduce the environmental impact of power production.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> LED lamps are even more efficient, and are free of mercury. They are regarded as the best solution in terms of cost effectiveness and robustness.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Efforts_to_improve_efficiency">Efforts to improve efficiency</span></h3>\n<p>Some research has been carried out to improve the efficacy of commercial incandescent lamps. In 2007, the consumer lighting division of <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> announced a "high efficiency incandescent" (HEI) lamp project, which they claimed would ultimately be as much as four times more efficient than current incandescents, although their initial production goal was to be approximately twice as efficient.<sup id="cite_ref-notsodim_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsodim-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The HEI program was terminated in 2008 due to slow progress.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>US Department of Energy research at <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> initially indicated the potential for dramatically improved efficiency from a <a href="/wiki/Photonic_lattice" class="mw-redirect" title="Photonic lattice">photonic lattice</a> filament.<sup id="cite_ref-notsodim_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsodim-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> However, later work indicated that initially promising results were in error.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prompted by legislation in various countries mandating increased bulb efficiency, new "hybrid" incandescent bulbs have been introduced by <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">Philips</a>. The "Halogena Energy Saver" incandescents can produce about 23 lm/W; about 30 percent more efficient than traditional incandescents, by using a reflective capsule to reflect formerly wasted infrared radiation back to the filament from which it can be re-emitted as visible light.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> This concept was pioneered by Duro-Test in 1980 with a commercial product that produced 29.8 lm/W.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> More advanced reflectors based on <a href="/wiki/Interference_filter" title="Interference filter">interference filters</a> or <a href="/wiki/Photonic_crystal" title="Photonic crystal">photonic crystals</a> can theoretically result in higher efficiency, up to a limit of about 270 lm/W (40% of the maximum efficacy possible).<sup id="cite_ref-Ilic_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ilic-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Laboratory proof-of-concept experiments have produced as much as 45 lm/W, approaching the efficacy of compact fluorescent bulbs.<sup id="cite_ref-Ilic_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ilic-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction">Construction</span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Incandescent_light_bulb&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(October 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Incandescent light bulbs consist of an air-tight glass enclosure (the envelope, or bulb) with a filament of <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> wire inside the bulb, through which an <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a> is passed. Contact wires and a base with two (or more) conductors provide electrical connections to the filament. Incandescent light bulbs usually contain a stem or glass mount anchored to the bulb\'s base that allows the electrical contacts to run through the envelope without air or gas leaks. Small wires embedded in the stem in turn support the filament and its lead wires.\n</p><p>An electric current heats the filament to typically 2,000 to 3,300&#160;K (3,140 to 5,480&#160;\xc2\xb0F), well below tungsten\'s melting point of 3,695&#160;K (6,191&#160;\xc2\xb0F). Filament temperatures depend on the filament type, shape, size, and amount of current drawn. The heated filament emits light that approximates a <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">continuous spectrum</a>. The useful part of the emitted energy is <a href="/wiki/Visible_light" class="mw-redirect" title="Visible light">visible light</a>, but most energy is given off as heat in the near-<a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> wavelengths.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/3-way_bulb" class="mw-redirect" title="3-way bulb">Three-way light bulbs</a> have two filaments and three conducting contacts in their bases. The filaments share a common ground, and can be lit separately or together. Common wattages include 30\xe2\x80\x9370\xe2\x80\x93100, 50\xe2\x80\x93100\xe2\x80\x93150, and 100\xe2\x80\x93200\xe2\x80\x93300, with the first two numbers referring to the individual filaments, and the third giving the combined wattage.\n</p><p>Most light bulbs have either clear or coated glass. The coated glass bulbs have a white powdery substance on the inside called <a href="/wiki/Kaolin" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaolin">kaolin</a>. Kaolin, or <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">kaolinite</a>, is a white, chalky clay in a very fine powder form, that is blown in and electrostatically deposited on the interior of the bulb. It diffuses the light emitted from the filament, producing a more gentle and evenly distributed light. Manufacturers may add pigments to the kaolin to adjust the characteristics of the final light emitted from the bulb. Kaolin diffused bulbs are used extensively in interior lighting because of their comparatively gentle light. Other kinds of colored bulbs are also made, including the various colors used for "party bulbs", <a href="/wiki/Christmas_tree" title="Christmas tree">Christmas tree</a> lights and other decorative lighting. These are created by <a href="/wiki/Glass_coloring_and_color_marking" title="Glass coloring and color marking">coloring the glass</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Dopant" title="Dopant">dopant</a>; which is often a metal like <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> (blue) or <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a> (green).<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Neodymium-containing glass is sometimes used to provide a more natural-appearing light.\n</p>\n<table border="0">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td>\n<div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Incandescent_light_bulb.svg" class="image"><img alt="Incandescent light bulb.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Incandescent_light_bulb.svg/280px-Incandescent_light_bulb.svg.png" width="280" height="360" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Incandescent_light_bulb.svg/420px-Incandescent_light_bulb.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Incandescent_light_bulb.svg/560px-Incandescent_light_bulb.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="2571" /></a></div></div>\n</td>\n<td>\n<ol><li>Outline of Glass bulb</li>\n<li>Low pressure inert gas (<a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">krypton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">Tungsten</a> filament</li>\n<li>Contact wire (goes out of stem)</li>\n<li>Contact wire (goes into stem)</li>\n<li>Support wires (one end embedded in stem; conduct no current)</li>\n<li>Stem (glass mount)</li>\n<li>Contact wire (goes out of stem)</li>\n<li>Cap (sleeve)</li>\n<li>Insulation (<a href="/wiki/Vitrite" title="Vitrite">vitrite</a>)</li>\n<li>Electrical contact</li></ol>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Many arrangements of electrical contacts are used. Large lamps may have a <a href="/wiki/Edison_screw" title="Edison screw">screw base</a> (one or more contacts at the tip, one at the shell) or a <a href="/wiki/Bayonet_mount" title="Bayonet mount">bayonet base</a> (one or more contacts on the base, shell used as a contact or used only as a mechanical support). Some tubular lamps have an electrical contact at either end. Miniature lamps may have a wedge base and wire contacts, and some automotive and special purpose lamps have screw terminals for connection to wires. Contacts in the lamp socket allow the electric current to pass through the base to the filament. Power ratings for incandescent light bulbs range from about 0.1 watt to about 10,000 watts.\n</p><p>The glass bulb of a general service lamp can reach temperatures between 200 and 260&#160;\xc2\xb0C (392 and 500&#160;\xc2\xb0F). Lamps intended for high power operation or used for heating purposes will have envelopes made of hard glass or <a href="/wiki/Fused_quartz" title="Fused quartz">fused quartz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lightbulb_socket" title="Lightbulb socket">Lightbulb socket</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_fill">Gas fill</span></h3>\n<p>Most modern bulbs are filled with an <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a> to reduce <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> of the filament and prevent its <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a>. The gas is at a pressure of about 70&#160;kPa (0.7&#160;atm).<sup id="cite_ref-argon1_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-argon1-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The role of the gas is to prevent evaporation of the filament, but the fill must be chosen carefully to avoid introducing significant heat losses. For these properties, chemical inertness and high <a href="/wiki/Relative_atomic_mass" title="Relative atomic mass">atomic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mass" title="Molecular mass">molecular weight</a> is desirable. The presence of gas molecules knocks the liberated tungsten atoms back to the filament,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources.
