(September 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> reducing its evaporation and allowing it to be operated at higher temperature without reducing its life (or, for operating at the same temperature, prolongs the filament life). On the other hand, the presence of the gas leads to heat loss from the filament\xe2\x80\x94and therefore efficiency loss due to reduced incandescence\xe2\x80\x94by <a href="/wiki/Heat_conduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat conduction">heat conduction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heat_convection" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat convection">heat convection</a>.\n</p><p>Early lamps, and some small modern lamps used only a vacuum to protect the filament from oxygen. The vacuum increases evaporation of the filament but eliminates two modes of heat loss.\n</p><p>The most commonly used fills are:<sup id="cite_ref-ChemArtLight_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChemArtLight-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">Vacuum</a>, used in small lamps. Provides best thermal insulation of the filament but does not protect against its evaporation. Used also in larger lamps where the outer bulb surface temperature has to be limited.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">Argon</a> (93%) and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> (7%), where argon is used for its inertness, low <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> and low cost, and the nitrogen is added to increase the breakdown voltage and prevent arcing between parts of the filament<sup id="cite_ref-argon1_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-argon1-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Nitrogen, used in some higher-power lamps, e.g. projection lamps, and where higher breakdown voltage is needed due to proximity of filament parts or lead-in wires</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">Krypton</a>, which is more advantageous than argon due to its higher atomic weight and lower thermal conductivity (which also allows use of smaller bulbs), but its use is hindered by much higher cost, confining it mostly to smaller-size bulbs.</li>\n<li>Krypton mixed with <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>, where xenon improves the gas properties further due to its higher atomic weight. Its use is however limited by its very high cost. The improvements by using xenon are modest in comparison to its cost.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">Hydrogen</a>, in special flashing lamps where rapid filament cooling is required; its high thermal conductivity is exploited here.</li></ul>\n<p>The gas fill must be free of traces of water. In the presence of the hot filament, water reacts with tungsten forming tungsten trioxide and atomic hydrogen. The oxide deposits on the bulb inner surface and reacts with hydrogen, decomposing to metallic tungsten and water. Water then cycles back to the filament. This greatly accelerates the bulb blackening, in comparison with evaporation-only.\n</p><p>The gas layer close to the filament (called the Langmuir layer) is stagnant, with heat transfer occurring only by conduction. Only at some distance does convection occur to carry heat to the bulb\'s envelope.\n</p><p>The orientation of the filament influences efficiency. Gas flow parallel to the filament, e.g., a vertically oriented bulb with vertical (or axial) filament, reduces convective losses.\n</p><p>The efficiency of the lamp increases with a larger filament diameter. Thin-filament, low-power bulbs benefit less from a fill gas, so are often only evacuated. In special cases, when rapid cooling of a filament is needed (e.g. in flashing lights), hydrogen gas fill is used.\n</p><p>Early lightbulbs with carbon filaments also used <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> vapor. However, carbon filaments operate at lower temperatures than tungsten ones, so the effect of the fill gas was not significant as the heat losses offset any benefits.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacturing">Manufacturing</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tantalum_light_bulb.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tantalum_light_bulb.png/170px-Tantalum_light_bulb.png" width="170" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tantalum_light_bulb.png/255px-Tantalum_light_bulb.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tantalum_light_bulb.png/340px-Tantalum_light_bulb.png 2x" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="1016" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tantalum_light_bulb.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1902 <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a> filament light bulb was the first one to have a metal filament. This one is from 1908.</div></div></div>\n<p>Early bulbs were laboriously assembled by hand. After automatic machinery was developed, the cost of bulbs fell. Until 1910, when Libbey\'s Westlake machine went into production, bulbs were generally produced by a team of three workers (two gatherers and a master gaffer) blowing the bulbs into wooden or cast-iron molds, coated with a paste.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Around 150 bulbs per hour were produced by the hand-blowing process in the 1880s at Corning Glass Works.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Westlake machine, developed by <a href="/wiki/Libbey_Glass" class="mw-redirect" title="Libbey Glass">Libbey Glass</a>, was based on an adaptation of the Owens-Libbey bottle-blowing machine. <a href="/wiki/Corning_Glass_Works" class="mw-redirect" title="Corning Glass Works">Corning Glass Works</a> soon began developing competing automated bulb-blowing machines, the first of which to be used in production was the E-Machine.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Corning continued developing automated bulb-production machines, installing the Ribbon Machine in 1926 in its <a href="/wiki/Wellsboro,_Pennsylvania" title="Wellsboro, Pennsylvania">Wellsboro</a>, Pennsylvania factory.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The Ribbon Machine surpassed any previous attempts to automate bulb production and was used to produce incandescent bulbs into the 21st century. The inventor, William Woods, along with his colleague at Corning Glass Works, David E. Gray, had created a machine that by 1939 was turning out 1,000 bulbs per minute.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Ribbon Machine works by passing a continuous ribbon of glass along a <a href="/wiki/Conveyor_belt" title="Conveyor belt">conveyor belt</a>, heated in a furnace, and then blown by precisely aligned air nozzles through holes in the conveyor belt into molds. Thus the glass bulbs or envelopes are created. A typical machine of this sort can produce anywhere from 50,000-120,000 bulbs per hour, depending on the size of the bulb.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> By the 1970s, 15 ribbon machines installed in factories around the world produced the entire supply of incandescent bulbs.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The filament and its supports are assembled on a glass stem, which is then.fused to the bulb. The air is pumped out of the bulb, and the evacuation tube in the stem press is sealed by a flame. The bulb is then inserted into the lamp base, and the whole assembly tested. The 2016 closing of <a href="/wiki/Osram_Sylvania" title="Osram Sylvania">Osram-Sylvania</a>\'s Wellsboro, Pennsylvania plant meant that one of the last remaining ribbon machines in the United States was shut down.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Filament">Filament</span></h2>\n<p>The first successful light bulb filaments were made of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> (from carbonized paper or <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a>). Early carbon filaments had a negative <a href="/wiki/Temperature_coefficient_of_resistivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperature coefficient of resistivity">temperature coefficient of resistance</a> \xe2\x80\x94 as they got hotter, their electrical resistance decreased. This made the lamp sensitive to fluctuations in the power supply, since a small increase of voltage would cause the filament to heat up, reducing its resistance and causing it to draw even more power and heat even further. In the "flashing" process, carbon filaments were heated by current passing through them while in an evacuated vessel containing hydrocarbon vapor (usually gasoline). The carbon deposited on the filament by this treatment improved the uniformity and strength of filaments as well as their efficiency. A metallized or "graphitized" filament was first heated in a high-temperature oven before flashing and lamp assembly. This transformed the carbon into <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, which further strengthened and smoothed the filament. This also changed the filament to have a positive temperature coefficient, like a metallic <a href="/wiki/Conductor_(electrical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conductor (electrical)">conductor</a>, and helped stabilize the lamp\'s power consumption, temperature and light output against minor variations in supply voltage.\n</p><p>In 1902, the <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> company developed a <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a> lamp filament. These lamps were more efficient than even graphitized carbon filaments and could operate at higher temperatures. Since tantalum metal has a lower resistivity than carbon, the tantalum lamp filament was quite long and required multiple internal supports. The metal filament had the property of gradually shortening in use; the filaments were installed with large loops that tightened in use. This made lamps in use for several hundred hours quite fragile.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Metal filaments had the property of breaking and re-welding, though this would usually decrease resistance and shorten the life of the filament. General Electric bought the rights to use tantalum filaments and produced them in the US until 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1898 to around 1905, <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a> was also used as a lamp filament in Europe, and the metal was so expensive that used broken lamps could be returned for partial credit.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> It could not be made for 110 V or 220 V so several lamps were wired in series for use on standard voltage circuits.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; data-height=&quot;240&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;291824&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv" style="width:220px;height:124px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv/220px--Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/14/Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>How a tungsten filament is made</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1904, the <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> filament was developed by Croatian inventors <a href="/wiki/Franjo_Hanaman" title="Franjo Hanaman">Franjo Hanaman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Just" title="Alexander Just">Alexander Just</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Tungsten metal was initially not available in a form that allowed it to be drawn into fine wires. Filaments made from sintered tungsten powder were quite fragile. By 1910, a process was developed by <a href="/wiki/William_D._Coolidge" title="William D. Coolidge">William D. Coolidge</a> at <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> for production of a ductile form of tungsten. The process required pressing tungsten powder into bars, then several steps of sintering, swaging, and then wire drawing. It was found that very pure tungsten formed filaments that sagged in use, and that a very small "doping" treatment with potassium, silicon, and aluminium oxides at the level of a few hundred parts per million greatly improved the life and durability of the tungsten filaments.<sup id="cite_ref-tungstendoping_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tungstendoping-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coiled_coil_filament">Coiled coil filament</span></h3>\n<p>To improve the efficiency of the lamp, the filament usually consists of multiple coils of coiled fine wire, also known as a \'coiled coil\'. Light bulbs using coiled coil filaments are sometimes referred to as \'double-coil bulbs\'. For a 60-watt 120-volt lamp, the uncoiled length of the tungsten filament is usually 22.8 inches (580&#160;mm),<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and the filament diameter is 0.0018 inches (0.046&#160;mm). The advantage of the coiled coil is that evaporation of the tungsten filament is at the rate of a tungsten cylinder having a diameter equal to that of the coiled coil. The coiled-coil filament evaporates more slowly than a straight filament of the same surface area and light-emitting power. As a result, the filament can then run hotter, which results in a more efficient light source, while reducing the evaporation so that the filament will last longer than a straight filament at the same temperature.\n</p><p>There are several different shapes of filament used in lamps, with differing characteristics. Manufacturers designate the types with codes such as C-6, CC-6, C-2V, CC-2V, C-8, CC-88, C-2F, CC-2F, C-Bar, C-Bar-6, C-8I, C-2R, CC-2R, and Axial.\n</p>\n<div class="mw-stack mobile-float-reset" style="box-sizing: border-box; float:right;"><div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Filament.jpg" incandescent lightbulb highly magnified</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fused_Electrical_Filament_SEM_Stereo_50x.JPG" title="Anaglyph 3D">3D red cyan</a> glasses are recommended to view this image correctly.</small></div></div></div>\n</div></div>\n<p>Electrical filaments are also used in <a href="/wiki/Hot_cathode" title="Hot cathode">hot cathodes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">vacuum tubes</a> as a source of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> or in vacuum tubes to heat an electron-emitting electrode.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reducing_filament_evaporation">Reducing filament evaporation</span></h3>\n<p>One of the problems of the standard electric light bulb is <i>filament notching</i> due to evaporation of the filament. Small variations in <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical resistivity">resistivity</a> along the filament cause "hot spots" to form at points of higher resistivity;<sup id="cite_ref-Kane2001_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kane2001-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> a variation of diameter of only 1% will cause a 25% reduction in service life.<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> These hot spots evaporate faster than the rest of the filament, which increases the resistance at that point\xe2\x80\x94this creates a <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> that ends in the familiar tiny gap in an otherwise healthy-looking filament. <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">Irving Langmuir</a> found that an inert gas, instead of vacuum, would retard evaporation. General service incandescent light bulbs over about 25 watts in rating are now filled with a mixture of mostly <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> or sometimes <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">krypton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Lamps operated on direct current develop random stairstep irregularities on the filament surface which may cut lifespan in half compared to AC operation; different alloys of tungsten and <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a> can be used to counteract the effect.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since a filament breaking in a gas-filled bulb can form an <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">electric arc</a>, which may spread between the terminals and draw very heavy current, intentionally thin lead-in wires or more elaborate protection devices are therefore often used as <a href="/wiki/Fuse_(electrical)" title="Fuse (electrical)">fuses</a> built into the light bulb.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> More nitrogen is used in higher-voltage lamps to reduce the possibility of arcing.\n</p><p>While inert gas reduces filament evaporation, it also conducts heat from the filament, thereby cooling the filament and reducing efficiency. At constant pressure and temperature, the thermal conductivity of a gas depends upon the molecular weight of the gas and the cross sectional area of the gas molecules. Higher molecular weight gasses have lower thermal conductivity, because both the molecular weight is higher and also the cross sectional area is higher. <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">Xenon</a> gas improves efficiency because of its high molecular weight, but is also more expensive, so its use is limited to smaller lamps.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During ordinary operation, the tungsten of the filament evaporates; hotter, more-efficient filaments evaporate faster. Because of this, the lifetime of a filament lamp is a trade-off between efficiency and longevity. The trade-off is typically set to provide a lifetime of several hundred to 2,000 hours for lamps used for general illumination. Theatrical, photographic, and projection lamps may have a useful life of only a few hours, trading life expectancy for high output in a compact form. Long-life general service lamps have lower efficiency but are used where the cost of changing the lamp is high compared to the value of energy used.\n</p><p>If a light bulb envelope leaks, the hot tungsten filament reacts with air, yielding an aerosol of brown <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_nitride" title="Tungsten nitride">tungsten nitride</a>, brown <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_dioxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Tungsten dioxide">tungsten dioxide</a>, violet-blue <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_pentoxide" title="Tungsten pentoxide">tungsten pentoxide</a>, and yellow <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_trioxide" title="Tungsten trioxide">tungsten trioxide</a> that then deposits on the nearby surfaces or the bulb interior.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulb_blackening">Bulb blackening</span></h3>\n<p>In a conventional lamp, the evaporated tungsten eventually condenses on the inner surface of the glass envelope, darkening it. For bulbs that contain a vacuum, the darkening is uniform across the entire surface of the envelope. When a filling of inert gas is used, the evaporated tungsten is carried in the thermal convection currents of the gas, depositing preferentially on the uppermost part of the envelope and blackening just that portion of the envelope. An incandescent lamp that gives 93% or less of its initial light output at 75% of its rated life is regarded as unsatisfactory, when tested according to IEC Publication 60064. Light loss is due to filament evaporation and bulb blackening.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Study of the problem of bulb blackening led to the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Thermionic_emission" title="Thermionic emission">Edison effect, thermionic emission</a> and invention of the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">vacuum tube</a>.\n</p><p>A very small amount of water vapor inside a light bulb can significantly affect lamp darkening. Water vapor <a href="/wiki/Thermolysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermolysis">dissociates</a> into hydrogen and oxygen at the hot filament. The oxygen attacks the tungsten metal, and the resulting tungsten oxide particles travel to cooler parts of the lamp. Hydrogen from water vapor reduces the oxide, reforming water vapor and continuing this <i>water cycle</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kane2001_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kane2001-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The equivalent of a drop of water distributed over 500,000 lamps will significantly increase darkening.<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Small amounts of substances such as <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a> are placed within the lamp as a <a href="/wiki/Getter" title="Getter">getter</a> to react with any oxygen that may bake out of the lamp components during operation.\n</p><p>Some old, high-powered lamps used in theater, projection, searchlight, and lighthouse service with heavy, sturdy filaments contained loose tungsten powder within the envelope. From time to time, the operator would remove the bulb and shake it, allowing the tungsten powder to scrub off most of the tungsten that had condensed on the interior of the envelope, removing the blackening and brightening the lamp again.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halogen_lamps">Halogen lamps</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG/220px-Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG" width="220" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG/330px-Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG/440px-Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1110" data-file-height="1125" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Close-up of a tungsten filament inside a <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">halogen lamp</a>. The two ring-shaped structures left and right are filament supports.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">Halogen lamp</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">halogen lamp</a> reduces uneven evaporation of the filament and eliminates darkening of the envelope by filling the lamp with a <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogen</a> gas at low pressure, rather than an inert gas. The <a href="/wiki/Halogen_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Halogen cycle">halogen cycle</a> increases the lifetime of the bulb and prevents its darkening by redepositing tungsten from the inside of the bulb back onto the filament. The halogen lamp can operate its filament at a higher temperature than a standard gas filled lamp of similar power without loss of operating life. Such bulbs are much smaller than normal incandescent bulbs, and are widely used where intense illumination is needed in a limited space. <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">Fiber-optic</a> lamps for <a href="/wiki/Optical_microscopy" class="mw-redirect" title="Optical microscopy">optical microscopy</a> is one typical application.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Incandescent_arc_lamps">Incandescent arc lamps</span></h3>\n<p>A variation of the incandescent lamp did not use a hot wire filament, but instead used an arc struck on a spherical bead electrode to produce heat. The electrode then became incandescent, with the arc contributing little to the light produced. Such lamps were used for projection or illumination for scientific instruments such as <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscopes</a>. These arc lamps ran on relatively low voltages and incorporated tungsten filaments to start ionization within the envelope. They provided the intense concentrated light of an <a href="/wiki/Arc_lamp" title="Arc lamp">arc lamp</a> but were easier to operate. Developed around 1915, these lamps were displaced by mercury and <a href="/wiki/Xenon_arc_lamp" title="Xenon arc lamp">xenon arc lamps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_characteristics">Electrical characteristics</span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Comparison of efficacy by power\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>\n</th>\n<th colspan="2">120 volt lamps<sup id="cite_ref-Wells2012_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wells2012-115">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th colspan="2">230 volt lamps<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Power (W)</th>\n<th>Output (<a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">lm</a>)</th>\n<th>Efficacy (lm/W)</th>\n<th>Output (<a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">lm</a>)</th>\n<th>Efficacy (lm/W)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>5</td>\n<td>25</td>\n<td>5</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>15</td>\n<td>110</td>\n<td>7.3</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>25</td>\n<td>200</td>\n<td>8.0</td>\n<td>230</td>\n<td>9.2\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>40</td>\n<td>500</td>\n<td>12.5</td>\n<td>430</td>\n<td>10.8\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>60</td>\n<td>850</td>\n<td>14.2</td>\n<td>730</td>\n<td>12.2\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>75</td>\n<td>1,200</td>\n<td>16.0</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>100</td>\n<td>1,700</td>\n<td>17.0</td>\n<td>1,380</td>\n<td>13.8\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>150</td>\n<td>2,850</td>\n<td>19.0</td>\n<td>2,220</td>\n<td>14.8\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>200</td>\n<td>3,900</td>\n<td>19.5</td>\n<td>3,150</td>\n<td>15.8\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>300</td>\n<td>6,200</td>\n<td>20.7</td>\n<td>5,000</td>\n<td>16.7\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>500</td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td>8,400</td>\n<td>16.8\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power">Power</span></h3>\n<p>Incandescent lamps are nearly pure resistive loads with a <a href="/wiki/Power_factor" title="Power factor">power factor</a> of 1. This means the actual power consumed (in watts) and the apparent power (in <a href="/wiki/Volt-ampere" title="Volt-ampere">volt-amperes</a>) are equal. Incandescent light bulbs are usually <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">marketed</a> according to the <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electrical power</a> consumed. This is measured in watts and depends mainly on the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical resistance">resistance</a> of the filament, which in turn depends mainly on the filament\'s length, thickness, and material. For two bulbs of the same voltage, type, color, and clarity, the higher-powered bulb gives more light.\n</p><p>The table shows the approximate typical output, in <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">lumens</a>, of standard incandescent light bulbs at various powers. Light output of a 230 V version is usually slightly less than that of a 120 V version. The lower current (higher voltage) filament is thinner and has to be operated at a slightly lower temperature for same life expectancy, and that reduces energy efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The lumen values for "soft white" bulbs will generally be slightly lower than for clear bulbs at the same power.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_and_resistance">Current and resistance</span></h3>\n<p>The actual resistance of the filament is temperature dependent. The cold resistance of tungsten-filament lamps is about 1/15 the hot-filament resistance when the lamp is operating. For example, a 100-watt, 120-volt lamp has a resistance of 144 <a href="/wiki/Ohm" title="Ohm">ohms</a> when lit, but the cold resistance is much lower (about 9.5 ohms).<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> Since incandescent lamps are resistive loads, simple phase-control <a href="/wiki/TRIAC" title="TRIAC">TRIAC</a> <a href="/wiki/Dimmer" title="Dimmer">dimmers</a> can be used to control brightness. Electrical contacts may carry a "T" rating symbol indicating that they are designed to control circuits with the high inrush current characteristic of tungsten lamps. For a 100-watt, 120-volt general-service lamp, the current stabilizes in about 0.10 seconds, and the lamp reaches 90% of its full brightness after about 0.13 seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriedelIsrael2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8U-Naf4DuzMCpgPA22_22\xe2\x80\x9323&#93;_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriedelIsrael2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8U-Naf4DuzMCpgPA22_22\xe2\x80\x9323]-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Carbon filament bulbs have the opposite characteristic.  The resistance of a carbon filament is higher when it is cold than when it is operating.  In the case of a 240&#160;Volt, 60&#160;Watt carbon filament bulb, the resistance of the filament when at operating temperature is 960&#160;Ohms, but rises to around 1500&#160;Ohms when cold.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulb_shapes">Bulb shapes</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg/440px-Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg.png" width="440" height="391" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg/660px-Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg/880px-Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Incandescent light bulbs come in a range of shapes and sizes.</div></div></div>\n<p>Incandescent light bulbs come in a range of shapes and sizes. The names of the shapes vary somewhat from region to regions. Many of these shapes have a designation consisting of one or more letters followed by one or more numbers, e.g. A55 or PAR38. The letters represent the shape of the bulb. The numbers represent the maximum diameter, either in &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>8</sub></span> of an inch, or in millimeters, depending on the shape and the region. For example, 63&#160;mm reflectors are designated R63, but in the US, they are known as R20 (2.5&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> However, in both regions, a PAR38 reflector is known as PAR38.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/ANSI" class="mw-redirect" title="ANSI">ANSI</a> C79.1-2002, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Standard">IS</a> 14897:2000<sup id="cite_ref-IS_14897:2000_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IS_14897:2000-121">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Industrial_Standards" title="Japanese Industrial Standards">JIS</a> C 7710:1988<sup id="cite_ref-JIS_C_7710:1988_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JIS_C_7710:1988-122">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> cover a common terminology for bulb shapes.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">Examples</span></h4>\n<table E26</td>\n<td>\xe2\x8c\x8060&#160;mm (~\xe2\x8c\x802.375") <a href="/wiki/A-series_light_bulb" title="A-series light bulb">A series bulb</a>, \xe2\x8c\x8026&#160;mm <a href="/wiki/Edison_screw" title="Edison screw">Edison screw</a><sup id="cite_ref-EU-screw_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EU-screw-123">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>candle-flame bulb</td>\n<td>CA35 E12</td>\n<td>CA11 E12</td>\n<td>\xe2\x8c\x8035&#160;mm (~\xe2\x8c\x801.375") candle-flame shape, \xe2\x8c\x8012&#160;mm Edison screw<sup id="cite_ref-EU-screw_123-1" class="reference"><a 5.33&#160;mm-spaced 12 V <a href="/wiki/Bi-pin_connector" class="mw-redirect" title="Bi-pin connector">bi-pin connector</a>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_shape_codes">Common shape codes</span></h3>\n<dl><dt>General Service</dt>\n<dd>Light emitted in (nearly) all directions. Available either clear or frosted.</dd>\n<dd>Types: <a href="/wiki/A-series_light_bulb" title="A-series light bulb">General</a> (A), Mushroom, elliptical (E), sign (S), tubular (T)</dd>\n<dd>120 V sizes: A17, 19 and 21</dd>\n<dd>230 V sizes: A55 and 60<sup id="cite_ref-EU-metric_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EU-metric-124">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<dl><dt>High Wattage General Service</dt>\n<dd>Lamps greater than 200 watts.</dd>\n<dd>Types: Pear-shaped (PS)</dd></dl>\n<dl><dt>Decorative</dt>\n<dd>lamps used in chandeliers, etc.</dd>\n<dd>Types: candle (B), twisted candle, bent-tip candle (CA &amp; BA), flame (F), globe (G), lantern chimney (H), fancy round (P)</dd>\n<dd>230 V sizes: P45, G95</dd></dl>\n<dl><dt>Reflector (R)</dt>\n<dd>Reflective coating inside the bulb directs light forward. Flood types (FL) spread light. Spot types (SP) concentrate the light. Reflector (R) bulbs put approximately double the amount of light (foot-candles) on the front central area as General Service (A) of same wattage.</dd>\n<dd>Types: Standard reflector (R), elliptical reflector (ER), crown-silvered</dd>\n<dd>120 V sizes: R16, 20, 25 and 30</dd>\n<dd>230 V sizes: R50, 63, 80 and 95<sup id="cite_ref-EU-metric_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EU-metric-124">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<dl><dt>Parabolic aluminized reflector (PAR)</dt>\n<dd><a href="/wiki/Parabolic_aluminized_reflector_light" title="Parabolic aluminized reflector light">Parabolic aluminized reflector</a> (PAR) bulbs control light more precisely. They produce about four times the concentrated light intensity of general service (A), and are used in recessed and track lighting. Weatherproof casings are available for outdoor spot and flood fixtures.</dd>\n<dd>120 V sizes: PAR 16, 20, 30, 38, 56 and 64</dd>\n<dd>230 V sizes: PAR 16, 20, 30, 38, 56 and 64</dd>\n<dd>Available in numerous spot and flood beam spreads. Like all light bulbs, the number represents the diameter of the bulb in &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>8</sub></span> of an inch. Therefore, a PAR 16 is 2&#160;in in diameter, a PAR 20 is 2.5&#160;in in diameter, PAR 30 is 3.75&#160;in and a PAR 38 is 4.75&#160;in in diameter.</dd></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GE_60_watt_package_light_bulbs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/GE_60_watt_package_light_bulbs.jpg/220px-GE_60_watt_package_light_bulbs.jpg" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/GE_60_watt_package_light_bulbs.jpg/330px-GE_60_watt_package_light_bulbs.jpg 1.5x, class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GE_60_watt_package_light_bulbs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A package of four 60 watt light bulbs</div></div></div>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Multifaceted_reflector" title="Multifaceted reflector">Multifaceted reflector</a> (MR)</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MR-Variations.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/MR-Variations.jpg/220px-MR-Variations.jpg" width="220" class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MR-Variations.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Left to right: MR16 with GU10 base, MR16 with GU5.3 base, MR11 with GU4 or GZ4 base</div></div></div>\n<dl><dt>HIR</dt>\n<dd>"HIR" is a <a href="/wiki/GE" class="mw-redirect" title="GE">GE</a> designation for a lamp with an infrared reflective coating. Since less heat escapes, the filament burns hotter and more efficiently.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Osram" title="Osram">Osram</a> designation for a similar coating is "IRC".<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lamp_bases">Lamp bases</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lightbulb_sockets" class="mw-redirect" title="Lightbulb sockets">Lightbulb sockets</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg/220px-Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg/330px-Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg/440px-Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>40-watt light bulbs with standard E10, E14 and E27 Edison screw base</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BCincandescentlamp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/BCincandescentlamp.jpg/170px-BCincandescentlamp.jpg" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/BCincandescentlamp.jpg/255px-BCincandescentlamp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/BCincandescentlamp.jpg/340px-BCincandescentlamp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="934" data-file-height="1189" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BCincandescentlamp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The double-contact bayonet cap on an incandescent bulb</div></div></div>\n<p>Very small lamps may have the filament support wires extended through the base of the lamp, and can be directly soldered to a printed circuit board for connections. Some reflector-type lamps include screw terminals for connection of wires. Most lamps have metal bases that fit in a socket to support the lamp and conduct current to the filament wires. In the late 19th century, manufacturers introduced a multitude of incompatible lamp bases. <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> introduced standard base sizes for tungsten incandescent lamps under the <a href="/wiki/Mazda_(light_bulb)" title="Mazda (light bulb)">Mazda</a> trademark in 1909. This standard was soon adopted across the US, and the Mazda name was used by many manufacturers under license through 1945. Today most incandescent lamps for general lighting service use an <a href="/wiki/Edison_screw" title="Edison screw">Edison screw</a> in candelabra, intermediate, or standard or mogul sizes, or double contact <a href="/wiki/Bayonet_mount" title="Bayonet mount">bayonet</a> base. Technical standards for lamp bases include ANSI standard C81.67 and IEC standard 60061-1 for common commercial lamp sizes, to ensure interchangeablitity between different manufacturer\'s products. Bayonet base lamps are frequently used in <a href="/wiki/Automotive_lamp_types" class="mw-redirect" title="Automotive lamp types">automotive lamps</a> to resist loosening due to vibration. A <a href="/wiki/Bipin" class="mw-redirect" title="Bipin">bipin</a> base is often used for halogen or reflector lamps.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lamp bases may be secured to the bulb with a cement, or by mechanical crimping to indentations molded into the glass bulb.\n</p><p>Miniature lamps used for some automotive lamps or decorative lamps have <a href="/wiki/Wedge_base" title="Wedge base">wedge bases</a> that have a partial plastic or even completely glass base. In this case, the wires wrap around to the outside of the bulb, where they press against the contacts in the socket. Miniature Christmas bulbs use a plastic wedge base as well.\n</p><p>Lamps intended for use in optical systems such as film projectors, microscope illuminators, or <a href="/wiki/Stage_lighting_instruments" class="mw-redirect" title="Stage lighting instruments">stage lighting instruments</a> have bases with alignment features so that the filament is positioned accurately within the optical system. A screw-base lamp may have a random orientation of the filament when the lamp is installed in the socket.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Light_output_and_lifetime">Light output and lifetime</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lamp_rerating" title="Lamp rerating">Lamp rerating</a></div>\n<p>Incandescent lamps are very sensitive to changes in the supply voltage. These characteristics are of great practical and economic importance.\n</p><p>For a supply voltage <i>V</i> near the rated voltage of the lamp:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Light</i> output is approximately proportional to <i>V</i><sup> 3.4</sup></li>\n<li><i>Power</i> consumption is approximately proportional to <i>V</i><sup> 1.6</sup></li>\n<li><i>Lifetime</i> is approximately proportional to <i>V</i><sup> \xe2\x88\x9216</sup></li>\n<li><i>Color temperature</i> is approximately proportional to <i>V</i><sup> 0.42</sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>This means that a 5% reduction in operating voltage will more than double the life of the bulb, at the expense of reducing its light output by about 16%. This may be a very acceptable trade off for a light bulb that is in a difficult-to-access location (for example, traffic lights or fixtures hung from high ceilings). Long-life bulbs take advantage of this trade-off. Since the value of the electric power they consume is much more than the value of the lamp, general service lamps emphasize efficiency over long operating life. The objective is to minimize the cost of light, not the cost of lamps.<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Early bulbs had a life of up to 2500 hours, but in 1924 a <a href="/wiki/Phoebus_cartel" title="Phoebus cartel">cartel</a> agreed to limit life to 1000 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> When this was exposed in 1953, General Electric and other leading American manufacturers were banned from limiting the life.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The relationships above are valid for only a few percent change of voltage around rated conditions, but they do indicate that a lamp operated at much lower than rated voltage could last for hundreds of times longer than at rated conditions, albeit with greatly reduced light output. The "<a href="/wiki/Centennial_Light" title="Centennial Light">Centennial Light</a>" is a light bulb that is accepted by the <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_Book_of_World_Records" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinness Book of World Records">Guinness Book of World Records</a></i> as having been burning almost continuously at a <a href="/wiki/Fire_station" title="Fire station">fire station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Livermore,_California" title="Livermore, California">Livermore, California</a>, since 1901. However, the bulb emits the equivalent light of a four watt bulb. A similar story can be told of a 40-watt bulb in Texas that has been illuminated since 21 September 1908. It once resided in an <a href="/wiki/Opera_house" title="Opera house">opera house</a> where notable celebrities stopped to take in its glow, and was moved to an area museum in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In flood lamps used for photographic lighting, the tradeoff is made in the other direction. Compared to general-service bulbs, for the same power, these bulbs produce far more light, and (more importantly) light at a higher color temperature, at the expense of greatly reduced life (which may be as short as two hours for a type P1 lamp). The upper temperature limit for the filament is the melting point of the metal. Tungsten is the metal with the highest melting point, 3,695&#160;K (6,191&#160;\xc2\xb0F). A 50-hour-life projection bulb, for instance, is designed to operate only 50&#160;\xc2\xb0C (122&#160;\xc2\xb0F) below that melting point. Such a lamp may achieve up to 22 lumens per watt, compared with 17.5 for a 750-hour general service lamp.<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lamps designed for different voltages have different luminous efficacy. For example, a 100-watt, 120-volt lamp will produce about 17.1 lumens per watt. A lamp with the same rated lifetime but designed for 230 V would produce only around 12.8 lumens per watt, and a similar lamp designed for 30 volts (train lighting) would produce as much as 19.8 lumens per watt.<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Lower voltage lamps have a thicker filament, for the same power rating. They can run hotter for the same lifetime before the filament evaporates.\n</p><p>The wires used to support the filament make it mechanically stronger, but remove heat, creating another tradeoff between efficiency and long life. Many general-service 120-volt lamps use no additional support wires, but lamps designed for "<a href="/wiki/Rough_service" class="mw-redirect" title="Rough service">rough service</a>" or "vibration service" may have as many as five. Low-voltage lamps have filaments made of heavier wire and do not require additional support wires.\n</p><p>Very low voltages are inefficient since the lead wires would conduct too much heat away from the filament, so the practical lower limit for incandescent lamps is 1.5 volts. Very long filaments for high voltages are fragile, and lamp bases become more difficult to insulate, so lamps for illumination are not made with rated voltages over 300 volts.<sup id="cite_ref-GETP110_62-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GETP110-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Some infrared heating elements are made for higher voltages, but these use tubular bulbs with widely separated terminals.\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg/133px-Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg" width="133" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg/200px-Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg/266px-Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1536" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Light" title="Centennial Light">Centennial Light</a> is the longest-lasting light bulb in the world.\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:41.5px Upper left: fluorescent lamp, upper right: incandescent bulb, lower left: white LED, lower right: candle flame.\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Crystal_energy.svg" class="image"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Crystal_energy.svg/29px-Crystal_energy.svg.png" width="29" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Crystal_energy.svg/44px-Crystal_energy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Crystal_energy.svg/59px-Crystal_energy.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="130" data-file-height="124" /></a></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Energy" title="Portal:Energy">Energy portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 18em; E. (1896). <i>Electric Incandescent Lighting</i>. New York: The W. J. Johnston Company. pp.&#160;18\xe2\x80\x9342  &#8211; via <a href="/wiki/Internet_Archive" title="Internet Archive">Internet Archive</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Electric+Incandescent+Lighting&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=18-42&amp;rft.pub=The+W.+J.+Johnston+Company&amp;rft.date=1896&amp;rft.aulast=Houston&amp;rft.aufirst=Edwin+J.&amp;rft.au=Kennely%2C+A.+E.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIncandescent+light+bulb" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="/wiki/Luminosity_function" title="Luminosity function">luminosity function</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Edison\'s research team was aware of the large negative <a href="/wiki/Temperature_coefficient_of_resistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperature coefficient of resistance">temperature coefficient of resistance</a> of possible lamp filament materials and worked extensively during the period 1878\xe2\x80\x931879 on devising an automatic regulator or <i>ballast</i> to stabilize current. It wasn\'t until 1879 that it was realized a self-limiting lamp could be built. See <cite id="CITEREFFriedelIsrael2010" class="citation book">Friedel, Robert &amp; Israel, Paul (2010). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=8U-Naf4DuzMC&amp;pg=PA26&amp;source=gbs_selected_pages&amp;cad=2#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false"><i>Edison\'s Electric Light: The Art of Invention</i></a> (Revised ed.). The Johns Hopkins University Press. pp.&#160;29\xe2\x80\x9331. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8018-9482-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8018-9482-4">978-0-8018-9482-4</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20171206142310/https://books.google.com/books?id=8U-Naf4DuzMC&amp;pg=PA7">Archived</a> from the original on 6 December 2017<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">3 July</span> 2018</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Edison%27s+Electric+Light%3A+The+Art+of+Invention&amp;rft.pages=29-31&amp;rft.edition=Revised&amp;rft.pub=The+Johns+Hopkins+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8018-9482-4&amp;rft.aulast=Friedel&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert&amp;rft.au=Israel%2C+Paul&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D8U-Naf4DuzMC%26pg%3DPA26%26source%3Dgbs_selected_pages%26cad%3D2%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIncandescent+light+bulb" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-EU-screw-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-EU-screw_123-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-EU-screw_123-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-EU-screw_123-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"> Instead of a 26&#160;mm E26 screw used for 110V, European 230V light bulbs use a 27&#160;mm (E27) screw. Likewise, European candle-flame bulbs use E14 instead of E12. See also <a href="/wiki/Edison_screw#Types" title="Edison screw">Edison screw types</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-EU-metric-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-EU-metric_124-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-EU-metric_124-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Size measured in millimeters.
